{"uri": "2024-07-411386602", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-04", "time": "14:46:35", "dateTime": "2024-07-04T14:46:35Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-04T14:30:00Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9803921580314636, "url": "https://muscatinejournal.com/life-entertainment/nation-world/music/woodstock-bethel-baby-boomers-new-york/article_18ad5a99-2f2c-552a-91a3-2f3cd22475ed.html", "title": "Back to Woodstock, with Wi-Fi: Women return after 55 years to glamp and relive the famous festival", "body": "BETHEL, N.Y. -- Beverly \"Cookie\" Grant hitchhiked to the Woodstock music festival in 1969 without a ticket and slept on straw. Ellen Shelburne arrived in a VW microbus and pitched a pup tent.\n\nFifty-five years later, the two longtime friends finally got back to the garden, but this time in high style.\n\nThe women, now 76, were recently treated to a two-bedroom glamping tent at the upstate New York site equipped with comfy beds, a shower, a coffee maker and Wi-Fi. No mud from drenching rains this time. They sat in pavilion seats to watch shows by Woodstock veterans John Fogerty and Roger Daltrey.\n\n\"We're like hippie queens!\" Grant joked over breakfast during the trip earlier this month.\n\nThe Bethel Woods Center for the Arts, the not-for-profit organization that runs the site, rolled out the tie-dyed carpet for Grant and Shelburne to promote its new luxury camping -- or glamping -- facilities and to delve deeper into Shelburne's trove of photos from the generation-defining festival held Aug. 15-18, 1969.\n\nThe once-trampled hillside by the main stage is now a manicured green space near a Woodstock-and-'60s-themed museum and the concert pavilion. But the return visit still bought back a flood of memories. Shelburne was able to retrace the steps she took as a 21-year-old college student in the photos taken by her then-boyfriend, and future husband, David Shelburne.\n\n\"I'm looking at this person in the photograph, who is me, but a person just starting out in life at that age. And now I'm looking back at sort of bookends of my life,\" Ellen Shelburne said. \"All these decades later, I'm back at Woodstock and it just brings it all up in such a positive way.\"\n\nGrant and Shelburne did not know each other in August 1969 and they attended the concert separately.\n\nShelburne came from Columbus, Ohio, with David Shelburne, his best friend and another woman. They purchased tickets, got there early and bought ponchos at a local store after rain was forecast. She slept in a pup tent.\n\n\"I was never cold, wet, hungry, muddy, dirty, uncomfortable or miserable,\" she said. \"It was the total opposite.\"\n\nGrant went to Woodstock on a lark.\n\nA long-haired surfer she knew named Ray came up to her and a friend on a beach in Fort Lauderdale, Florida, and said, \"There's this music festival happening in New York. You want to hitchhike up there with me?\" Grant's friend dropped out along the way, but she and the surfer made it to the town of Bethel. The last driver dropped them off at the edge of the epic traffic jam outside the festival and gave them a blanket.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | RSS Feed | SoundStack\n\nGrant walked the last several miles to Woodstock barefoot.\n\nBoth women were wowed by Jimi Hendrix, The Who and other musical acts, but also by the good vibes from the 400,000 or more people who converged on Max Yasgur's dairy farm some 80 miles northwest of New York City.\n\n\"If we needed food, someone gave us food. Someone gave us water. We needed nothing,\" Grant said.\n\nThe two women met months later in Columbus, where they each ran shops adjacent to Ohio State University with the men they went to Woodstock with. And they each married their concert companions, though Grant got a divorce several years later.\n\nDavid and Ellen Shelburne ran a film and video production company together until he died four years ago. Grant moved to Florida and eventually became a chef on mega-yachts before starting her own business providing crews for those big boats.\n\nEach woman kept a spark of the Woodstock spirit. Shelburne said she's \"stuck in the '60s and proud of it.\" They got the bug to return to the festival site last year after providing oral histories in Columbus to curators for the Museum at Bethel Woods.\n\nJust like in 1969, the women were provided what they needed during their recent long weekend of peace, love and nostalgia -- though this time it was a \"Luxury 2 Bedroom Safari Tent\" with a front deck and the shower in a bathroom. And when it rained this time, they were able to stay dry in the museum.\n\nOn a sunny Saturday, Bethel Woods senior curator Neal Hitch drove the women around in a golf cart to explore the spots where David Shelburne shot his festival photos. Unlike others who focused their cameras on the stage, he documented festivalgoers camping, swimming, selling goods, relaxing and having fun. Hitch noted that David Shelburne's images also are valuable because they are in sequence, meaning they tell a story.\n\nAt one stop, Shelburne stood by a tree line as she held a photo of a field full of campers. She was standing on the spot where her late husband took the photograph and was looking at the same field, minus the campers, 55 years later. Visibly moved, she said \"oh\" a few times and let out a deep breath before exclaiming, \"Wow!\"\n\nIt broke her heart that her husband is not in the photographs, but she felt his presence that weekend.\n\nThe women ranged across the festival site over several days, from the stage area to the woods where vendors had set up stalls. Despite the changes -- the luxury tents, the fences, the museum -- the women said they recognized the same mellow, friendly vibes here that they experienced as 21-year-olds.\n\nAnd they were thrilled to immerse themselves in it again decades later.\n\n\"It's very wonderful to see that it's in history forever,\" Grant said, \"and we're a part of that.\"\n\nWoodstock 55 years later: Camping out in luxury at iconic site", "source": {"uri": "muscatinejournal.com", "dataType": "news", "title": "Muscatine Journal"}, "authors": [{"uri": "associated_press@muscatinejournal.com", "name": "Associated Press", "type": "author", "isAgency": false}], "concepts": [{"uri": "http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant", "type": "person", "score": 5, "label": {"eng": "Ulysses S. Unlike others who focused their cameras on the stage, he documented festivalgoers camping, swimming, selling goods, relaxing and having fun. Hitch noted that David Shelburne's images also are valuable because they are in sequence, meaning they tell a story.\n\nAt one stop, Shelburne stood by a tree line as she held a photo of a field full of campers. She was standing on the spot where her late husband took the photograph and was looking at the same field, minus the campers, 55 years later. Ellen Shelburne arrived in a VW microbus and pitched a pup tent.\n\nFifty-five years later, the two longtime friends finally got back to the garden, but this time in high style.\n\nThe women, now 76, were recently treated to a two-bedroom glamping tent at the upstate New York site equipped with comfy beds, a shower, a coffee maker and Wi-Fi. No mud from drenching rains this time. They sat in pavilion seats to watch shows by Woodstock veterans John Fogerty and Roger Daltrey.\n\n\"We're like hippie queens!\" Grant joked over breakfast during the trip in June.\n\nThe Bethel Woods Center for the Arts, the not-for-profit organization that runs the site, rolled out the tie-dyed carpet for Grant and Shelburne to promote its new luxury camping -- or glamping -- facilities and to delve deeper into Shelburne's trove of photos from the generation-defining festival held Aug. 15-18, 1969.\n\nPeople are also reading... Marion drive in entrance design sparks controversy, extra look by officials PREP FOOTBALL: The VHSL master schedule was released on Thursday. Here are some games with intriguing storylines New Virginia Intermediate School is move-in ready EO prepares for grand opening of new facility COLLEGE BASEBALL: Bristol's Tudor brothers play behind the scenes role for Tennessee's national championship team Blood Eagle to offer unique, fine dining experience A new breakfast spot arrives in Bristol, Virginia EMORY & HENRY FOOTBALL: Harper has found a new home in Southwest Virginia LOCALS IN COLLEGE | Crash course in Charlottesville: Former Union standout Sneed enters second season as walk-on for Virginia MOTORSPORTS: Dillow brings diversity to Super Street field Perkins, the aging diner chain, has a 'new vibe' with a different name AUTO RACING: Wythe Raceway still a weekend destination for guys like Ken McMahan Bristol Motor Speedway hosts 4th Country Thunder Music Festival Dip Dog remains as popular as ever Races set for mayor and council seats in Chilhowie, Marion, Saltville\n\nThe once-trampled hillside by the main stage is now a manicured green space near a Woodstock-and-'60s-themed museum and the concert pavilion. But the return visit still brought back a flood of memories. Shelburne was able to retrace the steps she took as a 21-year-old college student in the photos taken by her then-boyfriend, and future husband, David Shelburne.\n\n\"I'm looking at this person in the photograph, who is me, but a person just starting out in life at that age. And now I'm looking back at sort of bookends of my life,\" Ellen Shelburne said. \"All these decades later, I'm back at Woodstock and it just brings it all up in such a positive way.\"\n\nGrant and Shelburne did not know each other in August 1969 and they attended the concert separately.\n\nShelburne came from Columbus, Ohio, with David Shelburne, his best friend and another woman. They purchased tickets, got there early and bought ponchos at a local store after rain was forecast. She slept in a pup tent.\n\n\"I was never cold, wet, hungry, muddy, dirty, uncomfortable or miserable,\" she said. \"It was the total opposite.\"\n\nGrant went to Woodstock on a lark.\n\nA long-haired surfer she knew named Ray came up to her and a friend on a beach in Fort Lauderdale, Florida, and said, \"There's this music festival happening in New York. You want to hitchhike up there with me?\" Grant's friend dropped out along the way, but she and the surfer made it to the town of Bethel. The last driver dropped them off at the edge of the epic traffic jam outside the festival and gave them a blanket.\n\nGrant walked the last several miles to Woodstock barefoot.\n\nBoth women were wowed by Jimi Hendrix, The Who and other musical acts, but also by the good vibes from the 400,000 or more people who converged on Max Yasgur's dairy farm some 80 miles northwest of New York City.\n\n\"If we needed food, someone gave us food. Someone gave us water. We needed nothing,\" Grant said.\n\nThe two women met months later in Columbus, where they each ran shops adjacent to Ohio State University with the men they went to Woodstock with. And they each married their concert companions, though Grant got a divorce several years later.\n\nDavid and Ellen Shelburne ran a film and video production company together until he died four years ago. Grant moved to Florida and eventually became a chef on mega-yachts before starting her own business providing crews for those big boats.\n\nEach woman kept a spark of the Woodstock spirit. Shelburne said she's \"stuck in the '60s and proud of it.\" They got the bug to return to the festival site last year after providing oral histories in Columbus to curators for the Museum at Bethel Woods.\n\nJust like in 1969, the women were provided what they needed during their recent long weekend of peace, love and nostalgia -- though this time it was a \"Luxury 2 Bedroom Safari Tent\" with a front deck and the shower in a bathroom. And when it rained this time, they were able to stay dry in the museum.\n\nOn a sunny Saturday, Bethel Woods senior curator Neal Hitch drove the women around in a golf cart to explore the spots where David Shelburne shot his festival photos. Unlike others who focused their cameras on the stage, he documented festivalgoers camping, swimming, selling goods, relaxing and having fun. Hitch noted that David Shelburne's images also are valuable because they are in sequence, meaning they tell a story.\n\nAt one stop, Shelburne stood by a tree line as she held a photo of a field full of campers. She was standing on the spot where her late husband took the photograph and was looking at the same field, minus the campers, 55 years later. Visibly moved, she said \"oh\" a few times and let out a deep breath before exclaiming, \"Wow!\"\n\nIt broke her heart that her husband is not in the photographs, but she felt his presence that weekend.\n\nThe women ranged across the festival site over several days, from the stage area to the woods where vendors had set up stalls. Despite the changes -- the luxury tents, the fences, the museum -- the women said they recognized the same mellow, friendly vibes here that they experienced as 21-year-olds.\n\nAnd they were thrilled to immerse themselves in it again decades later.\n\n\"It's very wonderful to see that it's in history forever,\" Grant said, \"and we're a part of that.\"\n\nBe the first to know\n\nGet local news delivered to your inbox!\n\nSign up! * I understand and agree that registration on or use of this site constitutes agreement to its user agreement and privacy policy.", "source": {"uri": "heraldcourier.com", "dataType": "news", "title": "HeraldCourier.com"}, "authors": [], "concepts": [{"uri": "http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glamping", "type": "wiki", "score": 5, "label": {"eng": "Glamping"}}, {"uri": "http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bethel_Woods_Center_for_the_Arts", "type": "wiki", "score": 5, "label": {"eng": "Bethel Woods Center for the Arts"}}, {"uri": "http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant", "type": "person", "score": 5, "label": {"eng": "Ulysses S. {"uri": "2024-07-409993096", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-03", "time": "12:09:29", "dateTime": "2024-07-03T12:09:29Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-03T11:32:29Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9607843160629272, "url": "https://wcfcourier.com/glamping-at-woodstock/article_11c67802-9993-5f63-bec5-627dba00dd81.html", "title": "GLAMPING AT WOODSTOCK", "body": "Ellen Shelburne and Beverly \"Cookie\" Grant sit in the original Woodstock Music and Art Fair tire swing June 14 at Bethel Woods Center for the Arts in Bethel, N.Y.\n\nMICHAEL HILL\n\nAssociated Press\n\n1969 REVISITED\n\nBETHEL, N.Y. -- Beverly \"Cookie\" Grant hitchhiked to the Woodstock music festival in 1969 without a ticket and slept on straw. Ellen Shelburne arrived in a VW microbus and pitched a pup tent.\n\nFifty-five years later, the two longtime friends finally got back to the garden, but this time in high style.\n\nThe women, now 76, were recently treated to a two-bedroom glamping tent at the upstate New York site equipped with comfy beds, a shower, a coffee maker and Wi-Fi. No mud from drenching rains this time. They sat in pavilion seats to watch shows by Woodstock veterans John Fogerty and Roger Daltrey.\n\n\"We're like hippie queens!\" Grant joked over breakfast during the trip in June.\n\nThe Bethel Woods Center for the Arts, the not-for-profit organization that runs the site, rolled out the tie-dyed carpet for Grant and Shelburne to promote its new luxury camping -- or glamping -- facilities and to delve deeper into Shelburne's trove of photos from the generation-defining festival held Aug. 15-18, 1969.\n\nPeople are also reading...\n\nChild shot while playing at Waterloo's Lafayette Park Sunday night NAME RELEASED: 2 Waterloo officers shot, suspect killed in Sunday shooting Deere has laid off nearly 1,000 since September, more expected today Patton Diner serving both unique and familiar dishes in Cedar Falls Several vehicles in 58 and Ridgeway crash John Deere closes on land for 1.2 million-square-foot Indiana warehouse Walnut Ridge Baptist Church plans move to new building, site plan change coming to council John Deere announces 600+ layoffs at East Moline, Davenport and Dubuque Jersey Mike's sandwich chain coming to Cedar Falls later this year Appointment of Waterloo police chief met with hesitation after officer involved shooting Suspect who shot two officers had spent time in prison for ramming a squad car in 2014 Man arrested in store robbery Inmate dies in prison serving time for New Hampton kidnapping, rape Minnesota man arrested for stealing purse from Cedar Falls vehicle College Football: UNI OC departs program, Panthers add coach to staff\n\nThe once-trampled hillside by the main stage is now a manicured green space near a Woodstock-and-'60s-themed museum and the concert pavilion. But the return visit still brought back a flood of memories. Shelburne was able to retrace the steps she took as a 21-year-old college student in the photos taken by her then-boyfriend, and future husband, David Shelburne.\n\n\"I'm looking at this person in the photograph, who is me, but a person just starting out in life at that age. And now I'm looking back at sort of bookends of my life,\" Ellen Shelburne said. \"All these decades later, I'm back at Woodstock and it just brings it all up in such a positive way.\"\n\nGrant and Shelburne did not know each other in August 1969 and they attended the concert separately.\n\nShelburne came from Columbus, Ohio, with David Shelburne, his best friend and another woman. They purchased tickets, got there early and bought ponchos at a local store after rain was forecast. She slept in a pup tent.\n\n\"I was never cold, wet, hungry, muddy, dirty, uncomfortable or miserable,\" she said. \"It was the total opposite.\"\n\nGrant went to Woodstock on a lark.\n\nA long-haired surfer she knew named Ray came up to her and a friend on a beach in Fort Lauderdale, Florida, and said, \"There's this music festival happening in New York. You want to hitchhike up there with me?\" Grant's friend dropped out along the way, but she and the surfer made it to the town of Bethel. The last driver dropped them off at the edge of the epic traffic jam outside the festival and gave them a blanket.\n\nGrant walked the last several miles to Woodstock barefoot.\n\nBoth women were wowed by Jimi Hendrix, The Who and other musical acts, but also by the good vibes from the 400,000 or more people who converged on Max Yasgur's dairy farm some 80 miles northwest of New York City.\n\n\"If we needed food, someone gave us food. Someone gave us water. We needed nothing,\" Grant said.\n\nThe two women met months later in Columbus, where they each ran shops adjacent to Ohio State University with the men they went to Woodstock with. And they each married their concert companions, though Grant got a divorce several years later.\n\nDavid and Ellen Shelburne ran a film and video production company together until he died four years ago. Grant moved to Florida and eventually became a chef on mega-yachts before starting her own business providing crews for those big boats.\n\nEach woman kept a spark of the Woodstock spirit. Shelburne said she's \"stuck in the '60s and proud of it.\" They got the bug to return to the festival site last year after providing oral histories in Columbus to curators for the Museum at Bethel Woods.\n\nJust like in 1969, the women were provided what they needed during their recent long weekend of peace, love and nostalgia -- though this time it was a \"Luxury 2 Bedroom Safari Tent\" with a front deck and the shower in a bathroom. And when it rained this time, they were able to stay dry in the museum.\n\nOn a sunny Saturday, Bethel Woods senior curator Neal Hitch drove the women around in a golf cart to explore the spots where David Shelburne shot his festival photos. Unlike others who focused their cameras on the stage, he documented festivalgoers camping, swimming, selling goods, relaxing and having fun. Hitch noted that David Shelburne's images also are valuable because they are in sequence, meaning they tell a story.\n\nAt one stop, Shelburne stood by a tree line as she held a photo of a field full of campers. She was standing on the spot where her late husband took the photograph and was looking at the same field, minus the campers, 55 years later. Visibly moved, she said \"oh\" a few times and let out a deep breath before exclaiming, \"Wow!\"\n\nIt broke her heart that her husband is not in the photographs, but she felt his presence that weekend.\n\nThe women ranged across the festival site over several days, from the stage area to the woods where vendors had set up stalls. Despite the changes -- the luxury tents, the fences, the museum -- the women said they recognized the same mellow, friendly vibes here that they experienced as 21-year-olds.\n\nAnd they were thrilled to immerse themselves in it again decades later.\n\n\"It's very wonderful to see that it's in history forever,\" Grant said, \"and we're a part of that.\"\n\nLove\n\n0\n\nFunny\n\n0\n\nWow\n\n0\n\nSad\n\n0\n\nAngry\n\n0\n\nBe the first to know\n\nGet local news delivered to your inbox!\n\nSign up!\n\n* I understand and agree that registration on or use of this site constitutes agreement to its user agreement and privacy policy.", "source": {"uri": "wcfcourier.com", "dataType": "news", "title": "Waterloo Cedar Falls Courier"}, "authors": [], "concepts": [{"uri": "http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glamping", "type": "wiki", "score": 5, "label": {"eng": "Glamping"}}, {"uri": "http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bethel_Woods_Center_for_the_Arts", "type": "wiki", "score": 5, "label": {"eng": "Bethel Woods Center for the Arts"}}, {"uri": "http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant", "type": "person", "score": 5, "label": {"eng": "Ulysses S. But the return visit still bought back a flood of memories. Shelburne was able to retrace the steps she took as a 21-year-old college student in the photos taken by her then-boyfriend, and future husband, David Shelburne.\n\n\"I'm looking at this person in the photograph, who is me, but a person just starting out in life at that age. And now I'm looking back at sort of bookends of my life,\" Ellen Shelburne said. \"All these decades later, I'm back at Woodstock and it just brings it all up in such a positive way.\"\n\nGrant and Shelburne did not know each other in August 1969 and they attended the concert separately.\n\nShelburne came from Columbus, Ohio, with David Shelburne, his best friend and another woman. They purchased tickets, got there early and bought ponchos at a local store after rain was forecast. She slept in a pup tent.\n\n\"I was never cold, wet, hungry, muddy, dirty, uncomfortable or miserable,\" she said. \"It was the total opposite.\"\n\nGrant went to Woodstock on a lark.\n\nA long-haired surfer she knew named Ray came up to her and a friend on a beach in Fort Lauderdale, Florida, and said, \"There's this music festival happening in New York. You want to hitchhike up there with me?\" Grant's friend dropped out along the way, but she and the surfer made it to the town of Bethel. The last driver dropped them off at the edge of the epic traffic jam outside the festival and gave them a blanket.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | RSS Feed | SoundStack\n\nGrant walked the last several miles to Woodstock barefoot.\n\nBoth women were wowed by Jimi Hendrix, The Who and other musical acts, but also by the good vibes from the 400,000 or more people who converged on Max Yasgur's dairy farm some 80 miles northwest of New York City.\n\n\"If we needed food, someone gave us food. Someone gave us water. We needed nothing,\" Grant said.\n\nThe two women met months later in Columbus, where they each ran shops adjacent to Ohio State University with the men they went to Woodstock with. And they each married their concert companions, though Grant got a divorce several years later.\n\nDavid and Ellen Shelburne ran a film and video production company together until he died four years ago. Grant moved to Florida and eventually became a chef on mega-yachts before starting her own business providing crews for those big boats.\n\nEach woman kept a spark of the Woodstock spirit. Shelburne said she's \"stuck in the '60s and proud of it.\" They got the bug to return to the festival site last year after providing oral histories in Columbus to curators for the Museum at Bethel Woods.\n\nJust like in 1969, the women were provided what they needed during their recent long weekend of peace, love and nostalgia -- though this time it was a \"Luxury 2 Bedroom Safari Tent\" with a front deck and the shower in a bathroom. And when it rained this time, they were able to stay dry in the museum.\n\nOn a sunny Saturday, Bethel Woods senior curator Neal Hitch drove the women around in a golf cart to explore the spots where David Shelburne shot his festival photos. Unlike others who focused their cameras on the stage, he documented festivalgoers camping, swimming, selling goods, relaxing and having fun. Hitch noted that David Shelburne's images also are valuable because they are in sequence, meaning they tell a story.\n\nAt one stop, Shelburne stood by a tree line as she held a photo of a field full of campers. She was standing on the spot where her late husband took the photograph and was looking at the same field, minus the campers, 55 years later. Visibly moved, she said \"oh\" a few times and let out a deep breath before exclaiming, \"Wow!\"\n\nIt broke her heart that her husband is not in the photographs, but she felt his presence that weekend.\n\nThe women ranged across the festival site over several days, from the stage area to the woods where vendors had set up stalls. Despite the changes -- the luxury tents, the fences, the museum -- the women said they recognized the same mellow, friendly vibes here that they experienced as 21-year-olds.\n\nAnd they were thrilled to immerse themselves in it again decades later.\n\n\"It's very wonderful to see that it's in history forever,\" Grant said, \"and we're a part of that.\"\n\nWoodstock 55 years later: Camping out in luxury at iconic site 0 Comments Love 0 Funny 0 Wow 0 Sad 0 Angry 0\n\nBe the first to know\n\nGet local news delivered to your inbox!\n\nSign up! * I understand and agree that registration on or use of this site constitutes agreement to its user agreement and privacy policy.", "source": {"uri": "wcfcourier.com", "dataType": "news", "title": "Waterloo Cedar Falls Courier"}, "authors": [{"uri": "associated_press@wcfcourier.com", "name": "Associated Press", "type": "author", "isAgency": true}, {"uri": "julia_nikhinson@wcfcourier.com", "name": "Julia Nikhinson", "type": "author", "isAgency": false}], "concepts": [{"uri": "http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glamping", "type": "wiki", "score": 5, "label": {"eng": "Glamping"}}, {"uri": "http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant", "type": "person", "score": 5, "label": {"eng": "Ulysses S. {"uri": "2024-07-409884670", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-03", "time": "10:40:30", "dateTime": "2024-07-03T10:40:30Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-03T10:05:45Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9529411792755127, "url": "https://siouxcityjournal.com/glamping-at-woodstock/article_61b20f08-edfd-55b6-b888-5439cad8bb1d.html", "title": "GLAMPING AT WOODSTOCK", "body": "Ellen shelburne and Beverly \"cookie\" Grant sit in the original Woodstock music and Art Fair tire swing June 14 at Bethel Woods center for the Arts in Bethel, N.Y.\n\nMICHAEL HILL\n\nAssociated Press\n\n1969 REVISITED\n\nBETHEL, N.Y. -- Beverly \"Cookie\" Grant hitchhiked to the Woodstock music festival in 1969 without a ticket and slept on straw. Ellen Shelburne arrived in a VW microbus and pitched a pup tent.\n\nFifty-five years later, the two longtime friends finally got back to the garden, but this time in high style.\n\nThe women, now 76, were recently treated to a two-bedroom glamping tent at the upstate New York site equipped with comfy beds, a shower, a coffee maker and Wi-Fi. No mud from drenching rains this time. They sat in pavilion seats to watch shows by Woodstock veterans John Fogerty and Roger Daltrey.\n\n\"We're like hippie queens!\" Grant joked over breakfast during the trip in June.\n\nThe Bethel Woods Center for the Arts, the not-for-profit organization that runs the site, rolled out the tie-dyed carpet for Grant and Shelburne to promote its new luxury camping -- or glamping -- facilities and to delve deeper into Shelburne's trove of photos from the generation-defining festival held Aug. 15-18, 1969.\n\nPeople are also reading...\n\nOkoboji lakes remain at near record levels; water activities still curtailed as July 4 holiday nears Panel looks to release more water from Okoboji lakes without hurting flood-stricken Spencer, Cherokee Sergeant Bluff-Luton assistant principal, AD resigns days after being placed on administrative leave Where to go for firework displays in Siouxland McCook Lake faces questions of flood recovery, future mitigation Dairy plant sues Sanborn over loss of $4 million in cheese What to know before you go to Saturday in the Park 2024 Siouxland homes without flood insurance face coverage limits and costly repairs after flooding McCook Lake residents voice their flooding concerns to Sen. Mike Rounds Cherokee residents try to stave off despair by jumping into action after flood OUR OPINION: Noem erred by not having National Guard help with S.D. flooding Rock Valley police chief confirms flood-related death Man accused of vandalizing 12 vehicles in Sioux City parking ramp Million gallons of sewage flowing into Missouri River per day from Sioux City wastewater plant High school baseball top performers: East's Colling, RSM's Loucks, MOC's McCarty drive teams' push\n\nThe once-trampled hillside by the main stage is now a manicured green space near a Woodstock-and-'60s-themed museum and the concert pavilion. But the return visit still brought back a flood of memories. Shelburne was able to retrace the steps she took as a 21-year-old college student in the photos taken by her then-boyfriend, and future husband, David Shelburne.\n\n\"I'm looking at this person in the photograph, who is me, but a person just starting out in life at that age. And now I'm looking back at sort of bookends of my life,\" Ellen Shelburne said. \"All these decades later, I'm back at Woodstock and it just brings it all up in such a positive way.\"\n\nGrant and Shelburne did not know each other in August 1969 and they attended the concert separately.\n\nShelburne came from Columbus, Ohio, with David Shelburne, his best friend and another woman. They purchased tickets, got there early and bought ponchos at a local store after rain was forecast. She slept in a pup tent.\n\n\"I was never cold, wet, hungry, muddy, dirty, uncomfortable or miserable,\" she said. \"It was the total opposite.\"\n\nGrant went to Woodstock on a lark.\n\nA long-haired surfer she knew named Ray came up to her and a friend on a beach in Fort Lauderdale, Florida, and said, \"There's this music festival happening in New York. You want to hitchhike up there with me?\" Grant's friend dropped out along the way, but she and the surfer made it to the town of Bethel. The last driver dropped them off at the edge of the epic traffic jam outside the festival and gave them a blanket.\n\nGrant walked the last several miles to Woodstock barefoot.\n\nBoth women were wowed by Jimi Hendrix, The Who and other musical acts, but also by the good vibes from the 400,000 or more people who converged on Max Yasgur's dairy farm some 80 miles northwest of New York City.\n\n\"If we needed food, someone gave us food. Someone gave us water. We needed nothing,\" Grant said.\n\nThe two women met months later in Columbus, where they each ran shops adjacent to Ohio State University with the men they went to Woodstock with. And they each married their concert companions, though Grant got a divorce several years later.\n\nDavid and Ellen Shelburne ran a film and video production company together until he died four years ago. Grant moved to Florida and eventually became a chef on mega-yachts before starting her own business providing crews for those big boats.\n\nEach woman kept a spark of the Woodstock spirit. Shelburne said she's \"stuck in the '60s and proud of it.\" They got the bug to return to the festival site last year after providing oral histories in Columbus to curators for the Museum at Bethel Woods.\n\nJust like in 1969, the women were provided what they needed during their recent long weekend of peace, love and nostalgia -- though this time it was a \"Luxury 2 Bedroom Safari Tent\" with a front deck and the shower in a bathroom. And when it rained this time, they were able to stay dry in the museum.\n\nOn a sunny Saturday, Bethel Woods senior curator Neal Hitch drove the women around in a golf cart to explore the spots where David Shelburne shot his festival photos. Unlike others who focused their cameras on the stage, he documented festivalgoers camping, swimming, selling goods, relaxing and having fun. Hitch noted that David Shelburne's images also are valuable because they are in sequence, meaning they tell a story.\n\nAt one stop, Shelburne stood by a tree line as she held a photo of a field full of campers. She was standing on the spot where her late husband took the photograph and was looking at the same field, minus the campers, 55 years later. Visibly moved, she said \"oh\" a few times and let out a deep breath before exclaiming, \"Wow!\" It broke her heart that her husband is not in the photographs, but she felt his presence that weekend.\n\nThe women ranged across the festival site over several days, from the stage area to the woods where vendors had set up stalls. Despite the changes -- the luxury tents, the fences, the museum -- the women said they recognized the same mellow, friendly vibes here that they experienced as 21-year-olds.\n\nAnd they were thrilled to immerse themselves in it again decades later.\n\n\"It's very wonderful to see that it's in history forever,\" Grant said, \"and we're a part of that.\"\n\nLove\n\n0\n\nFunny\n\n0\n\nWow\n\n0\n\nSad\n\n0\n\nAngry\n\n0\n\nBe the first to know\n\nGet local news delivered to your inbox!\n\nSign up!\n\n* I understand and agree that registration on or use of this site constitutes agreement to its user agreement and privacy policy.", "source": {"uri": "siouxcityjournal.com", "dataType": "news", "title": "Sioux City Journal"}, "authors": [], "concepts": [{"uri": "http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glamping", "type": "wiki", "score": 5, "label": {"eng": "Glamping"}}, {"uri": "http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bethel_Woods_Center_for_the_Arts", "type": "wiki", "score": 5, "label": {"eng": "Bethel Woods Center for the Arts"}}, {"uri": "http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant", "type": "person", "score": 5, "label": {"eng": "Ulysses S. Shelburne said she's \"stuck in the '60s and proud of it.\" They got the bug to return to the festival site last year after providing oral histories in Columbus to curators for the Museum at Bethel Woods.\n\nJust like in 1969, the women were provided what they needed during their recent long weekend of peace, love and nostalgia -- though this time it was a \"Luxury 2 Bedroom Safari Tent\" with a front deck and the shower in a bathroom. And when it rained this time, they were able to stay dry in the museum.\n\nOn a sunny Saturday, Bethel Woods senior curator Neal Hitch drove the women around in a golf cart to explore the spots where David Shelburne shot his festival photos. Unlike others who focused their cameras on the stage, he documented festivalgoers camping, swimming, selling goods, relaxing and having fun. Hitch noted that David Shelburne's images also are valuable because they are in sequence, meaning they tell a story.\n\nAt one stop, Shelburne stood by a tree line as she held a photo of a field full of campers. She was standing on the spot where her late husband took the photograph and was looking at the same field, minus the campers, 55 years later. Visibly moved, she said \"oh\" a few times and let out a deep breath before exclaiming, \"Wow!\"\n\nIt broke her heart that her husband is not in the photographs, but she felt his presence that weekend.\n\nThe women ranged across the festival site over several days, from the stage area to the woods where vendors had set up stalls. Despite the changes -- the luxury tents, the fences, the museum -- the women said they recognized the same mellow, friendly vibes here that they experienced as 21-year-olds.\n\nAnd they were thrilled to immerse themselves in it again decades later.\n\n\"It's very wonderful to see that it's in history forever,\" Grant said, \"and we're a part of that.\"\n\nLove 0 Funny 0 Wow 0 Sad 0 Angry 0\n\nBe the first to know\n\nGet local news delivered to your inbox!\n\nSign up! * I understand and agree that registration on or use of this site constitutes agreement to its user agreement and privacy policy.", "source": {"uri": "pantagraph.com", "dataType": "news", "title": "pantagraph.com"}, "authors": [], "concepts": [{"uri": "http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bethel_Woods_Center_for_the_Arts", "type": "wiki", "score": 5, "label": {"eng": "Bethel Woods Center for the Arts"}}, {"uri": "http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant", "type": "person", "score": 5, "label": {"eng": "Ulysses S. Ellen Shelburne arrived in a VW microbus and pitched a pup tent.\n\nFifty-five years later, the two longtime friends finally got back to the garden, but this time in high style.\n\nThe women, now 76, were recently treated to a two-bedroom glamping tent at the upstate New York site equipped with comfy beds, a shower, a coffee maker and Wi-Fi. No mud from drenching rains this time. They sat in pavilion seats to watch shows by Woodstock veterans John Fogerty and Roger Daltrey.\n\n\"We're like hippie queens!\" Grant joked over breakfast during the trip in June.\n\nThe Bethel Woods Center for the Arts, the not-for-profit organization that runs the site, rolled out the tie-dyed carpet for Grant and Shelburne to promote its new luxury camping -- or glamping -- facilities and to delve deeper into Shelburne's trove of photos from the generation-defining festival held Aug. 15-18, 1969.\n\nPeople are also reading... But the return visit still brought back a flood of memories. Shelburne was able to retrace the steps she took as a 21-year-old college student in the photos taken by her then-boyfriend, and future husband, David Shelburne.\n\n\"I'm looking at this person in the photograph, who is me, but a person just starting out in life at that age. And now I'm looking back at sort of bookends of my life,\" Ellen Shelburne said. \"All these decades later, I'm back at Woodstock and it just brings it all up in such a positive way.\"\n\nGrant and Shelburne did not know each other in August 1969 and they attended the concert separately.\n\nShelburne came from Columbus, Ohio, with David Shelburne, his best friend and another woman. They purchased tickets, got there early and bought ponchos at a local store after rain was forecast. She slept in a pup tent.\n\n\"I was never cold, wet, hungry, muddy, dirty, uncomfortable or miserable,\" she said. \"It was the total opposite.\"\n\nGrant went to Woodstock on a lark.\n\nA long-haired surfer she knew named Ray came up to her and a friend on a beach in Fort Lauderdale, Florida, and said, \"There's this music festival happening in New York. You want to hitchhike up there with me?\" Grant's friend dropped out along the way, but she and the surfer made it to the town of Bethel. The last driver dropped them off at the edge of the epic traffic jam outside the festival and gave them a blanket.\n\nGrant walked the last several miles to Woodstock barefoot.\n\nBoth women were wowed by Jimi Hendrix, The Who and other musical acts, but also by the good vibes from the 400,000 or more people who converged on Max Yasgur's dairy farm some 80 miles northwest of New York City.\n\n\"If we needed food, someone gave us food. Someone gave us water. We needed nothing,\" Grant said.\n\nThe two women met months later in Columbus, where they each ran shops adjacent to Ohio State University with the men they went to Woodstock with. And they each married their concert companions, though Grant got a divorce several years later.\n\nDavid and Ellen Shelburne ran a film and video production company together until he died four years ago. Grant moved to Florida and eventually became a chef on mega-yachts before starting her own business providing crews for those big boats.\n\nEach woman kept a spark of the Woodstock spirit. Shelburne said she's \"stuck in the '60s and proud of it.\" They got the bug to return to the festival site last year after providing oral histories in Columbus to curators for the Museum at Bethel Woods.\n\nJust like in 1969, the women were provided what they needed during their recent long weekend of peace, love and nostalgia -- though this time it was a \"Luxury 2 Bedroom Safari Tent\" with a front deck and the shower in a bathroom. And when it rained this time, they were able to stay dry in the museum.\n\nOn a sunny Saturday, Bethel Woods senior curator Neal Hitch drove the women around in a golf cart to explore the spots where David Shelburne shot his festival photos. Unlike others who focused their cameras on the stage, he documented festivalgoers camping, swimming, selling goods, relaxing and having fun. Hitch noted that David Shelburne's images also are valuable because they are in sequence, meaning they tell a story.\n\nAt one stop, Shelburne stood by a tree line as she held a photo of a field full of campers. She was standing on the spot where her late husband took the photograph and was looking at the same field, minus the campers, 55 years later. Visibly moved, she said \"oh\" a few times and let out a deep breath before exclaiming, \"Wow!\" It broke her heart that her husband is not in the photographs, but she felt his presence that weekend.\n\nThe women ranged across the festival site over several days, from the stage area to the woods where vendors had set up stalls. Ellen Shelburne arrived in a VW microbus and pitched a pup tent.\n\nFifty-five years later, the two longtime friends finally got back to the garden, but this time in high style.\n\nThe women, now 76, were recently treated to a two-bedroom glamping tent at the upstate New York site equipped with comfy beds, a shower, a coffee maker and Wi-Fi. No mud from drenching rains this time. They sat in pavilion seats to watch shows by Woodstock veterans John Fogerty and Roger Daltrey.\n\n\"We're like hippie queens!\" Grant joked over breakfast during the trip earlier this month.\n\nThe Bethel Woods Center for the Arts, the not-for-profit organization that runs the site, rolled out the tie-dyed carpet for Grant and Shelburne to promote its new luxury camping -- or glamping -- facilities and to delve deeper into Shelburne's trove of photos from the generation-defining festival held Aug. 15-18, 1969.\n\nPeople are also reading... McLean County correctional officer fired, charged with sexual misconduct involving inmate Flick Fact: How these three Illinois brothers became mega-millionaires? Departing Bloomington-Normal priests look to past, future Crystal Lake man identified in fatal crash near Downs Bloomington man pleads guilty to battering an officer Dr. Ashvin K. Patel Flick Fact: Besides Chicago, what other Illinois city once had an NBA franchise? Rivian deal with Volkswagen raises prospects for Georgia plant Flick Fact: What company employs so many in Illinois, it'd be one of our largest cities? Ag-focused tech hub in 'Silicorn Valley' Central Illinois awarded $51M federal grant Bloomington nonprofit at risk of relocation without new roof Make Music Normal finds the beat for 10th year uptown Heartland Community College announces new head cross country coach Aaron Rossi sentenced to 5 years in prison on federal fraud case Supreme Court declines to hear Illinois assault weapons ban challenge\n\nThe once-trampled hillside by the main stage is now a manicured green space near a Woodstock-and-'60s-themed museum and the concert pavilion. But the return visit still bought back a flood of memories. Shelburne was able to retrace the steps she took as a 21-year-old college student in the photos taken by her then-boyfriend, and future husband, David Shelburne.\n\n\"I'm looking at this person in the photograph, who is me, but a person just starting out in life at that age. And now I'm looking back at sort of bookends of my life,\" Ellen Shelburne said. \"All these decades later, I'm back at Woodstock and it just brings it all up in such a positive way.\"\n\nGrant and Shelburne did not know each other in August 1969 and they attended the concert separately.\n\nShelburne came from Columbus, Ohio, with David Shelburne, his best friend and another woman. They purchased tickets, got there early and bought ponchos at a local store after rain was forecast. She slept in a pup tent.\n\n\"I was never cold, wet, hungry, muddy, dirty, uncomfortable or miserable,\" she said. \"It was the total opposite.\"\n\nGrant went to Woodstock on a lark.\n\nA long-haired surfer she knew named Ray came up to her and a friend on a beach in Fort Lauderdale, Florida, and said, \"There's this music festival happening in New York. You want to hitchhike up there with me?\" Grant's friend dropped out along the way, but she and the surfer made it to the town of Bethel. The last driver dropped them off at the edge of the epic traffic jam outside the festival and gave them a blanket.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | RSS Feed | SoundStack\n\nGrant walked the last several miles to Woodstock barefoot.\n\nBoth women were wowed by Jimi Hendrix, The Who and other musical acts, but also by the good vibes from the 400,000 or more people who converged on Max Yasgur's dairy farm some 80 miles northwest of New York City.\n\n\"If we needed food, someone gave us food. Someone gave us water. We needed nothing,\" Grant said.\n\nThe two women met months later in Columbus, where they each ran shops adjacent to Ohio State University with the men they went to Woodstock with. And they each married their concert companions, though Grant got a divorce several years later.\n\nDavid and Ellen Shelburne ran a film and video production company together until he died four years ago. Grant moved to Florida and eventually became a chef on mega-yachts before starting her own business providing crews for those big boats.\n\nEach woman kept a spark of the Woodstock spirit. Shelburne said she's \"stuck in the '60s and proud of it.\" They got the bug to return to the festival site last year after providing oral histories in Columbus to curators for the Museum at Bethel Woods.\n\nJust like in 1969, the women were provided what they needed during their recent long weekend of peace, love and nostalgia -- though this time it was a \"Luxury 2 Bedroom Safari Tent\" with a front deck and the shower in a bathroom. And when it rained this time, they were able to stay dry in the museum.\n\nOn a sunny Saturday, Bethel Woods senior curator Neal Hitch drove the women around in a golf cart to explore the spots where David Shelburne shot his festival photos. Unlike others who focused their cameras on the stage, he documented festivalgoers camping, swimming, selling goods, relaxing and having fun. Hitch noted that David Shelburne's images also are valuable because they are in sequence, meaning they tell a story.\n\nAt one stop, Shelburne stood by a tree line as she held a photo of a field full of campers. She was standing on the spot where her late husband took the photograph and was looking at the same field, minus the campers, 55 years later. Visibly moved, she said \"oh\" a few times and let out a deep breath before exclaiming, \"Wow!\"\n\nIt broke her heart that her husband is not in the photographs, but she felt his presence that weekend.\n\nThe women ranged across the festival site over several days, from the stage area to the woods where vendors had set up stalls. Despite the changes -- the luxury tents, the fences, the museum -- the women said they recognized the same mellow, friendly vibes here that they experienced as 21-year-olds.\n\nAnd they were thrilled to immerse themselves in it again decades later.\n\n\"It's very wonderful to see that it's in history forever,\" Grant said, \"and we're a part of that.\"\n\nWoodstock 55 years later: Camping out in luxury at iconic site Love 0 Funny 0 Wow 0 Sad 0 Angry 0\n\nBe the first to know\n\nGet local news delivered to your inbox!\n\nSign up! * I understand and agree that registration on or use of this site constitutes agreement to its user agreement and privacy policy.", "source": {"uri": "pantagraph.com", "dataType": "news", "title": "pantagraph.com"}, "authors": [{"uri": "associated_press@pantagraph.com", "name": "Associated Press", "type": "author", "isAgency": false}, {"uri": "julia_nikhinson@pantagraph.com", "name": "Julia Nikhinson", "type": "author", "isAgency": false}], "concepts": [{"uri": "http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glamping", "type": "wiki", "score": 5, "label": {"eng": "Glamping"}}, {"uri": "http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant", "type": "person", "score": 5, "label": {"eng": "Ulysses S. {"uri": "8209735516", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-04", "time": "14:46:42", "dateTime": "2024-07-04T14:46:42Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-04T14:46:01Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9411764740943909, "url": "https://siouxcityjournal.com/life-entertainment/nation-world/music/woodstock-bethel-baby-boomers-new-york/article_0554c5cc-afe8-5def-ac26-5a315ebf8208.html", "title": "Back to Woodstock, with Wi-Fi: Women return after 55 years to glamp and relive the famous festival", "body": "BETHEL, N.Y. -- Beverly \"Cookie\" Grant hitchhiked to the Woodstock music festival in 1969 without a ticket and slept on straw. Ellen Shelburne arrived in a VW microbus and pitched a pup tent.\n\nFifty-five years later, the two longtime friends finally got back to the garden, but this time in high style.\n\nThe women, now 76, were recently treated to a two-bedroom glamping tent at the upstate New York site equipped with comfy beds, a shower, a coffee maker and Wi-Fi. No mud from drenching rains this time. They sat in pavilion seats to watch shows by Woodstock veterans John Fogerty and Roger Daltrey.\n\n\"We're like hippie queens!\" Grant joked over breakfast during the trip earlier this month.\n\nThe Bethel Woods Center for the Arts, the not-for-profit organization that runs the site, rolled out the tie-dyed carpet for Grant and Shelburne to promote its new luxury camping -- or glamping -- facilities and to delve deeper into Shelburne's trove of photos from the generation-defining festival held Aug. 15-18, 1969.\n\nPeople are also reading... Okoboji lakes remain at near record levels; water activities still curtailed as July 4 holiday nears Panel looks to release more water from Okoboji lakes without hurting flood-stricken Spencer, Cherokee Sergeant Bluff-Luton assistant principal, AD resigns days after being placed on administrative leave Where to go for firework displays in Siouxland McCook Lake faces questions of flood recovery, future mitigation Dairy plant sues Sanborn over loss of $4 million in cheese What to know before you go to Saturday in the Park 2024 Siouxland homes without flood insurance face coverage limits and costly repairs after flooding OUR OPINION: Noem erred by not having National Guard help with S.D. flooding High school baseball top performers: East's Colling, RSM's Loucks, MOC's McCarty drive teams' push Saturday in the Park continues to bring in big name acts without charging admission Noem: North Sioux 'tried to make the best decision' to protect homes, families Cherokee residents try to stave off despair by jumping into action after flood Some McCook Lake residents allowed access to flooded homes McCook Lake residents voice their flooding concerns to Sen. Mike Rounds\n\nThe once-trampled hillside by the main stage is now a manicured green space near a Woodstock-and-'60s-themed museum and the concert pavilion. But the return visit still bought back a flood of memories. Shelburne was able to retrace the steps she took as a 21-year-old college student in the photos taken by her then-boyfriend, and future husband, David Shelburne.\n\n\"I'm looking at this person in the photograph, who is me, but a person just starting out in life at that age. And now I'm looking back at sort of bookends of my life,\" Ellen Shelburne said. \"All these decades later, I'm back at Woodstock and it just brings it all up in such a positive way.\"\n\nGrant and Shelburne did not know each other in August 1969 and they attended the concert separately.\n\nShelburne came from Columbus, Ohio, with David Shelburne, his best friend and another woman. They purchased tickets, got there early and bought ponchos at a local store after rain was forecast. She slept in a pup tent.\n\n\"I was never cold, wet, hungry, muddy, dirty, uncomfortable or miserable,\" she said. \"It was the total opposite.\"\n\nGrant went to Woodstock on a lark.\n\nA long-haired surfer she knew named Ray came up to her and a friend on a beach in Fort Lauderdale, Florida, and said, \"There's this music festival happening in New York. You want to hitchhike up there with me?\" Grant's friend dropped out along the way, but she and the surfer made it to the town of Bethel. The last driver dropped them off at the edge of the epic traffic jam outside the festival and gave them a blanket.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | RSS Feed | SoundStack\n\nGrant walked the last several miles to Woodstock barefoot.\n\nBoth women were wowed by Jimi Hendrix, The Who and other musical acts, but also by the good vibes from the 400,000 or more people who converged on Max Yasgur's dairy farm some 80 miles northwest of New York City.\n\n\"If we needed food, someone gave us food. Someone gave us water. We needed nothing,\" Grant said.\n\nThe two women met months later in Columbus, where they each ran shops adjacent to Ohio State University with the men they went to Woodstock with. And they each married their concert companions, though Grant got a divorce several years later.\n\nDavid and Ellen Shelburne ran a film and video production company together until he died four years ago. Grant moved to Florida and eventually became a chef on mega-yachts before starting her own business providing crews for those big boats.\n\nEach woman kept a spark of the Woodstock spirit. Shelburne said she's \"stuck in the '60s and proud of it.\" They got the bug to return to the festival site last year after providing oral histories in Columbus to curators for the Museum at Bethel Woods.\n\nJust like in 1969, the women were provided what they needed during their recent long weekend of peace, love and nostalgia -- though this time it was a \"Luxury 2 Bedroom Safari Tent\" with a front deck and the shower in a bathroom. And when it rained this time, they were able to stay dry in the museum.\n\nOn a sunny Saturday, Bethel Woods senior curator Neal Hitch drove the women around in a golf cart to explore the spots where David Shelburne shot his festival photos. Unlike others who focused their cameras on the stage, he documented festivalgoers camping, swimming, selling goods, relaxing and having fun. Hitch noted that David Shelburne's images also are valuable because they are in sequence, meaning they tell a story.\n\nAt one stop, Shelburne stood by a tree line as she held a photo of a field full of campers. She was standing on the spot where her late husband took the photograph and was looking at the same field, minus the campers, 55 years later. Visibly moved, she said \"oh\" a few times and let out a deep breath before exclaiming, \"Wow!\"\n\nIt broke her heart that her husband is not in the photographs, but she felt his presence that weekend.\n\nThe women ranged across the festival site over several days, from the stage area to the woods where vendors had set up stalls. Despite the changes -- the luxury tents, the fences, the museum -- the women said they recognized the same mellow, friendly vibes here that they experienced as 21-year-olds.\n\nAnd they were thrilled to immerse themselves in it again decades later.\n\n\"It's very wonderful to see that it's in history forever,\" Grant said, \"and we're a part of that.\"\n\nWoodstock 55 years later: Camping out in luxury at iconic site 0 Comments Love 0 Funny 0 Wow 0 Sad 0 Angry 0\n\nBe the first to know\n\nGet local news delivered to your inbox!\n\nSign up! * I understand and agree that registration on or use of this site constitutes agreement to its user agreement and privacy policy.", "source": {"uri": "siouxcityjournal.com", "dataType": "news", "title": "Sioux City Journal"}, "authors": [{"uri": "associated_press@siouxcityjournal.com", "name": "Associated Press", "type": "author", "isAgency": true}, {"uri": "julia_nikhinson@siouxcityjournal.com", "name": "Julia Nikhinson", "type": "author", "isAgency": false}], "concepts": [{"uri": "http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/McCook_Lake", "type": "wiki", "score": 5, "label": {"eng": "McCook Lake"}}, {"uri": "http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glamping", "type": "wiki", "score": 5, "label": {"eng": "Glamping"}}, {"uri": "http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant", "type": "person", "score": 5, "label": {"eng": "Ulysses S. Ellen Shelburne arrived in a VW microbus and pitched a pup tent.\n\nFifty-five years later, the two longtime friends finally got back to the garden, but this time in high style.\n\nThe women, now 76, were recently treated to a two-bedroom glamping tent at the upstate New York site equipped with comfy beds, a shower, a coffee maker and Wi-Fi. No mud from drenching rains this time. They sat in pavilion seats to watch shows by Woodstock veterans John Fogerty and Roger Daltrey.\n\n\"We're like hippie queens!\" Grant joked over breakfast during the trip earlier this month.\n\nThe Bethel Woods Center for the Arts, the not-for-profit organization that runs the site, rolled out the tie-dyed carpet for Grant and Shelburne to promote its new luxury camping -- or glamping -- facilities and to delve deeper into Shelburne's trove of photos from the generation-defining festival held Aug. 15-18, 1969.\n\nPeople are also reading... Moline Post Office supervisor charged for allegedly stealing mail containing gift cards, cash John Deere announces 600+ layoffs at East Moline, Davenport and Dubuque JDC: Two top players out of PGA Tour field Sarah Goodwin says her husband's life was 'swirling out of control' before he was killed John Deere closes on land for 1.2 million-square-foot warehouse in Indiana Iowa restaurant that wanted to help teens fined for federal labor law violations PV standout and Cross-Fit athlete named 2024 Beat the Elite Runners Quad-Cities prepare for 18-foot Mississippi River flood Quad-Cities business openings, closings: Octeen Energy, Smith's Custom Meats 'There is quiet where he used to be.' We tried the new Whitey's flavor, available only at the John Deere Classic\n\nThe once-trampled hillside by the main stage is now a manicured green space near a Woodstock-and-'60s-themed museum and the concert pavilion. But the return visit still bought back a flood of memories. Shelburne was able to retrace the steps she took as a 21-year-old college student in the photos taken by her then-boyfriend, and future husband, David Shelburne.\n\n\"I'm looking at this person in the photograph, who is me, but a person just starting out in life at that age. And now I'm looking back at sort of bookends of my life,\" Ellen Shelburne said. \"All these decades later, I'm back at Woodstock and it just brings it all up in such a positive way.\"\n\nGrant and Shelburne did not know each other in August 1969 and they attended the concert separately.\n\nShelburne came from Columbus, Ohio, with David Shelburne, his best friend and another woman. They purchased tickets, got there early and bought ponchos at a local store after rain was forecast. She slept in a pup tent.\n\n\"I was never cold, wet, hungry, muddy, dirty, uncomfortable or miserable,\" she said. \"It was the total opposite.\"\n\nGrant went to Woodstock on a lark.\n\nA long-haired surfer she knew named Ray came up to her and a friend on a beach in Fort Lauderdale, Florida, and said, \"There's this music festival happening in New York. You want to hitchhike up there with me?\" Grant's friend dropped out along the way, but she and the surfer made it to the town of Bethel. The last driver dropped them off at the edge of the epic traffic jam outside the festival and gave them a blanket.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | RSS Feed | SoundStack\n\nGrant walked the last several miles to Woodstock barefoot.\n\nBoth women were wowed by Jimi Hendrix, The Who and other musical acts, but also by the good vibes from the 400,000 or more people who converged on Max Yasgur's dairy farm some 80 miles northwest of New York City.\n\n\"If we needed food, someone gave us food. Someone gave us water. We needed nothing,\" Grant said.\n\nThe two women met months later in Columbus, where they each ran shops adjacent to Ohio State University with the men they went to Woodstock with. And they each married their concert companions, though Grant got a divorce several years later.\n\nDavid and Ellen Shelburne ran a film and video production company together until he died four years ago. Grant moved to Florida and eventually became a chef on mega-yachts before starting her own business providing crews for those big boats.\n\nEach woman kept a spark of the Woodstock spirit. Shelburne said she's \"stuck in the '60s and proud of it.\" They got the bug to return to the festival site last year after providing oral histories in Columbus to curators for the Museum at Bethel Woods.\n\nJust like in 1969, the women were provided what they needed during their recent long weekend of peace, love and nostalgia -- though this time it was a \"Luxury 2 Bedroom Safari Tent\" with a front deck and the shower in a bathroom. And when it rained this time, they were able to stay dry in the museum.\n\nOn a sunny Saturday, Bethel Woods senior curator Neal Hitch drove the women around in a golf cart to explore the spots where David Shelburne shot his festival photos. Unlike others who focused their cameras on the stage, he documented festivalgoers camping, swimming, selling goods, relaxing and having fun. Hitch noted that David Shelburne's images also are valuable because they are in sequence, meaning they tell a story.\n\nAt one stop, Shelburne stood by a tree line as she held a photo of a field full of campers. She was standing on the spot where her late husband took the photograph and was looking at the same field, minus the campers, 55 years later. Visibly moved, she said \"oh\" a few times and let out a deep breath before exclaiming, \"Wow!\"\n\nIt broke her heart that her husband is not in the photographs, but she felt his presence that weekend.\n\nThe women ranged across the festival site over several days, from the stage area to the woods where vendors had set up stalls. Despite the changes -- the luxury tents, the fences, the museum -- the women said they recognized the same mellow, friendly vibes here that they experienced as 21-year-olds.\n\nAnd they were thrilled to immerse themselves in it again decades later.\n\n\"It's very wonderful to see that it's in history forever,\" Grant said, \"and we're a part of that.\"\n\nWoodstock 55 years later: Camping out in luxury at iconic site 0 Comments Love 0 Funny 0 Wow 0 Sad 0 Angry 0\n\nBe the first to know\n\nGet local news delivered to your inbox!\n\nSign up! * I understand and agree that registration on or use of this site constitutes agreement to its user agreement and privacy policy.", "source": {"uri": "qctimes.com", "dataType": "news", "title": "The Quad-City Times"}, "authors": [{"uri": "associated_press@qctimes.com", "name": "Associated Press", "type": "author", "isAgency": true}, {"uri": "julia_nikhinson@qctimes.com", "name": "Julia Nikhinson", "type": "author", "isAgency": false}], "concepts": [{"uri": "http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glamping", "type": "wiki", "score": 5, "label": {"eng": "Glamping"}}, {"uri": "http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant", "type": "person", "score": 5, "label": {"eng": "Ulysses S. Ellen Shelburne arrived in a VW microbus and pitched a pup tent.\n\nFifty-five years later, the two longtime friends finally got back to the garden, but this time in high style.\n\nThe women, now 76, were recently treated to a two-bedroom glamping tent at the upstate New York site equipped with comfy beds, a shower, a coffee maker and Wi-Fi. No mud from drenching rains this time. They sat in pavilion seats to watch shows by Woodstock veterans John Fogerty and Roger Daltrey.\n\n\"We're like hippie queens!\" Grant joked over breakfast during the trip earlier this month.\n\nThe Bethel Woods Center for the Arts, the not-for-profit organization that runs the site, rolled out the tie-dyed carpet for Grant and Shelburne to promote its new luxury camping -- or glamping -- facilities and to delve deeper into Shelburne's trove of photos from the generation-defining festival held Aug. 15-18, 1969.\n\nPeople are also reading... Man found dead near Lake James in McDowell County Plans underway for Jimmy Carter's 100th birthday Man dies after jumping from pickup truck in Glen Alpine; driver charged Body found Sunday was Marion man; cause of death pending toxicology report Marion man faces drug charges following search of home When are the 4th of July fireworks, parades in McDowell County? Rotary Club of Marion installs new officers, presents annual awards Marion man charged with fentanyl possession Abby Wyatt, three others from McDowell softball earn All-State honors Video: Ultralight crashes near Morganton after hitting tree McDowell Titans release 2024 varsity football schedule McDowell Children's Theatre presents kids musical Man arrested for stealing vehicle, wallet of man who died in Old Fort McDowell county tax rate remains the same, water fees up 10% Adventures with Dandy: Reporter, wife, dog enjoy Marion Wing Factory\n\nThe once-trampled hillside by the main stage is now a manicured green space near a Woodstock-and-'60s-themed museum and the concert pavilion. But the return visit still bought back a flood of memories. Shelburne was able to retrace the steps she took as a 21-year-old college student in the photos taken by her then-boyfriend, and future husband, David Shelburne.\n\n\"I'm looking at this person in the photograph, who is me, but a person just starting out in life at that age. And now I'm looking back at sort of bookends of my life,\" Ellen Shelburne said. \"All these decades later, I'm back at Woodstock and it just brings it all up in such a positive way.\"\n\nGrant and Shelburne did not know each other in August 1969 and they attended the concert separately.\n\nShelburne came from Columbus, Ohio, with David Shelburne, his best friend and another woman. They purchased tickets, got there early and bought ponchos at a local store after rain was forecast. She slept in a pup tent.\n\n\"I was never cold, wet, hungry, muddy, dirty, uncomfortable or miserable,\" she said. \"It was the total opposite.\"\n\nGrant went to Woodstock on a lark.\n\nA long-haired surfer she knew named Ray came up to her and a friend on a beach in Fort Lauderdale, Florida, and said, \"There's this music festival happening in New York. You want to hitchhike up there with me?\" Grant's friend dropped out along the way, but she and the surfer made it to the town of Bethel. The last driver dropped them off at the edge of the epic traffic jam outside the festival and gave them a blanket.\n\nListen now and subscribe: Apple Podcasts | Google Podcasts | Spotify | RSS Feed | SoundStack\n\nGrant walked the last several miles to Woodstock barefoot.\n\nBoth women were wowed by Jimi Hendrix, The Who and other musical acts, but also by the good vibes from the 400,000 or more people who converged on Max Yasgur's dairy farm some 80 miles northwest of New York City.\n\n\"If we needed food, someone gave us food. Someone gave us water. We needed nothing,\" Grant said.\n\nThe two women met months later in Columbus, where they each ran shops adjacent to Ohio State University with the men they went to Woodstock with. And they each married their concert companions, though Grant got a divorce several years later.\n\nDavid and Ellen Shelburne ran a film and video production company together until he died four years ago. Grant moved to Florida and eventually became a chef on mega-yachts before starting her own business providing crews for those big boats.\n\nEach woman kept a spark of the Woodstock spirit. Shelburne said she's \"stuck in the '60s and proud of it.\" They got the bug to return to the festival site last year after providing oral histories in Columbus to curators for the Museum at Bethel Woods.\n\nJust like in 1969, the women were provided what they needed during their recent long weekend of peace, love and nostalgia -- though this time it was a \"Luxury 2 Bedroom Safari Tent\" with a front deck and the shower in a bathroom. And when it rained this time, they were able to stay dry in the museum.\n\nOn a sunny Saturday, Bethel Woods senior curator Neal Hitch drove the women around in a golf cart to explore the spots where David Shelburne shot his festival photos. Unlike others who focused their cameras on the stage, he documented festivalgoers camping, swimming, selling goods, relaxing and having fun. Hitch noted that David Shelburne's images also are valuable because they are in sequence, meaning they tell a story.\n\nAt one stop, Shelburne stood by a tree line as she held a photo of a field full of campers. She was standing on the spot where her late husband took the photograph and was looking at the same field, minus the campers, 55 years later. Visibly moved, she said \"oh\" a few times and let out a deep breath before exclaiming, \"Wow!\"\n\nIt broke her heart that her husband is not in the photographs, but she felt his presence that weekend.\n\nThe women ranged across the festival site over several days, from the stage area to the woods where vendors had set up stalls. Despite the changes -- the luxury tents, the fences, the museum -- the women said they recognized the same mellow, friendly vibes here that they experienced as 21-year-olds.\n\nAnd they were thrilled to immerse themselves in it again decades later.\n\n\"It's very wonderful to see that it's in history forever,\" Grant said, \"and we're a part of that.\"\n\nWoodstock 55 years later: Camping out in luxury at iconic site Be the first to know\n\nGet local news delivered to your inbox!\n\nSign up! * I understand and agree that registration on or use of this site constitutes agreement to its user agreement and privacy policy.", "source": {"uri": "mcdowellnews.com", "dataType": "news", "title": "McDowellNews.com"}, "authors": [{"uri": "associated_press@mcdowellnews.com", "name": "Associated Press", "type": "author", "isAgency": false}, {"uri": "julia_nikhinson@mcdowellnews.com", "name": "Julia Nikhinson", "type": "author", "isAgency": false}], "concepts": [{"uri": "http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glamping", "type": "wiki", "score": 5, "label": {"eng": "Glamping"}}, {"uri": "http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant", "type": "person", "score": 5, "label": {"eng": "Ulysses S. {"uri": "8207717088", "lang": "eng", "isDuplicate": false, "date": "2024-07-03", "time": "12:00:25", "dateTime": "2024-07-03T12:00:25Z", "dateTimePub": "2024-07-03T11:56:11Z", "dataType": "news", "sim": 0.9372549057006836, "url": "https://napavalleyregister.com/glamping-at-woodstock/article_d7b5202e-dc99-5abb-a60a-99bf0003f81d.html", "title": "GLAMPING AT WOODSTOCK", "body": "Tents are set up June 14 at Bethel Woods Center for the Arts, the site of Woodstock Music and Art Fair.\n\nMICHAEL HILL\n\nAssociated Press\n\n1969 REVISITED\n\nBETHEL, N.Y. -- Beverly \"Cookie\" Grant hitchhiked to the Woodstock music festival in 1969 without a ticket and slept on straw. Ellen Shelburne arrived in a VW microbus and pitched a pup tent.\n\nFifty-five years later, the two long time friends finally got back to the garden, but this time in high style.\n\nThe women, now 76, were recently treated to a two-bedroom glamping tent at the upstate New York site equipped with comfy beds, a shower, a coffee maker and Wi-Fi. No mud from drenching rains this time. They sat in pavilion seats to watch shows by Woodstock veterans John Fogerty and Roger Daltrey.\n\n\"We're like hippie queens!\" Grant joked over breakfast during the trip in June.\n\nThe Bethel Woods Center for the Arts, the not-for-profit organization that runs the site, rolled out the tie-dyed carpet for Grant and Shelburne to promote its new luxury camping -- or glamping -- facilities and to delve deeper into Shelburne's trove of photos from the generation-defining festival held Aug. 15-18, 1969.\n\nPeople are also reading... Supervisor Belia Ramos files court documents alleging harassment Treasury Wine Estates hit with Napa lawsuit claiming labor violations Update: Toll Fire containment near Calistoga reaches 15%; evacuation orders lifted Live music at Friday Nights in the Park returns to downtown Napa Four arrested after Napa grocery robbery, police say Former American Canyon school principal charged after ex-teacher's sex abuse arrest Battle of the Bands coming to downtown Napa Manslaughter case over cyclist couple killed in Napa moves forward Learn where to celebrate the Fourth of July in the Napa Valley Document at center of Supervisor Ramos' restraining order found in public filings Update: Crews contain vegetation fire near Angwin The Kid LAROI and a Napa fan met and sang at BottleRock. Video will appear on CNN on July Fourth. NBA Draft: Prolific Prep of Napa Christian has two draftees in same year for first time Napa Police: Man arrested after sexual assault Concept for Napa's Fuller Park playable art structure is revealed\n\nThe once-trampled hillside by the main stage is now a manicured green space near a Woodstock-and-'60s-themed museum and the concert pavilion. But the return visit still brought back a flood of memories. Shelburne was able to retrace the steps she took as a 21-year-old college student in the photos taken by her then-boyfriend, and future husband, David Shelburne.\n\n\"I'm looking at this person in the photograph, who is me, but a person just starting out in life at that age. And now I'm looking back at sort of bookends of my life,\" Ellen Shelburne said. \"All these decades later, I'm back at Woodstock and it just brings it all up in such a positive way.\"\n\nGrant and Shelburne did not know each other in August 1969 and they attended the concert separately.\n\nShelburne came from Columbus, Ohio, with David Shelburne, his best friend and another woman. They purchased tickets, got there early and bought ponchos at a local store after rain was forecast. She slept in a pup tent.\n\n\"I was never cold, wet, hungry, muddy, dirty, uncomfortable or miserable,\" she said. \"It was the total opposite.\"\n\nGrant went to Woodstock on a lark.\n\nA long-haired surfer she knew named Ray came up to her and a friend on a beach in Fort Lauderdale, Florida, and said, \"There's this music festival happening in New York. You want to hitchhike up there with me?\" Grant's friend dropped out along the way, but she and the surfer made it to the town of Bethel. The last driver dropped them off at the edge of the epic traffic jam outside the festival and gave them a blanket.\n\nGrant walked the last several miles to Woodstock barefoot.\n\nBoth women were wowed by Jimi Hendrix, The Who and other musical acts, but also by the good vibes from the 400,000 or more people who converged on Max Yasgur's dairy farm some 80 miles northwest of New York City.\n\n\"If we needed food, someone gave us food. Someone gave us water. We needed nothing,\" Grant said.\n\nThe two women met months later in Columbus, where they each ran shops adjacent to Ohio State University with the men they went to Woodstock with. And they each married their concert companions, though Grant got a divorce several years later.\n\nDavid and Ellen Shelburne ran a film and video production company together until he died four years ago. Grant moved to Florida and eventually became a chef on mega-yachts before starting her own business providing crews for those big boats.\n\nEach woman kept a spark of the Woodstock spirit. Shelburne said she's \"stuck in the '60s and proud of it.\" They got the bug to return to the festival site last year after providing oral histories in Columbus to curators for the Museum at Bethel Woods.\n\nJust like in 1969, the women were provided what they needed during their recent long weekend of peace, love and nostalgia -- though this time it was a \"Luxury 2 Bedroom Safari Tent\" with a front deck and the shower in a bathroom. And when it rained this time, they were able to stay dry in the museum.\n\nOn a sunny Saturday, Bethel Woods senior curator Neal Hitch drove the women around in a golf cart to explore the spots where David Shelburne shot his festival photos. Unlike others who focused their cameras on the stage, he documented festivalgoers camping, swimming, selling goods, relaxing and having fun. Hitch noted that David Shelburne's images also are valuable because they are in sequence, meaning they tell a story.\n\nAt one stop, Shelburne stood by a tree line as she held a photo of a field full of campers. She was standing on the spot where her late husband took the photograph and was looking at the same field, minus the campers, 55 years later. Visibly moved, she said \"oh\" a few times and let out a deep breath before exclaiming, \"Wow!\" It broke her heart that her husband is not in the photographs, but she felt his presence that weekend.\n\nThe women ranged across the festival site over several days, from the stage area to the woods where vendors had set up stalls. Despite the changes -- the luxury tents, the fences, the museum -- the women said they recognized the same mellow, friendly vibes here that they experienced as 21-year-olds.\n\nAnd they were thrilled to immerse themselves in it again decades later.\n\n\"It's very wonderful to see that it's in history forever,\" Grant said, \"and we're a part of that.\"\n\nBe the first to know\n\nGet local news delivered to your inbox!\n\nSign up! * I understand and agree that registration on or use of this site constitutes agreement to its user agreement and privacy policy.", "source": {"uri": "napavalleyregister.com", "dataType": "news", "title": "Napa Valley Register"}, "authors": [], "concepts": [{"uri": "http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glamping", "type": "wiki", "score": 5, "label": {"eng": "Glamping"}}, {"uri": "http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bethel_Woods_Center_for_the_Arts", "type": "wiki", "score": 5, "label": {"eng": "Bethel Woods Center for the Arts"}}, {"uri": "http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant", "type": "person", "score": 5, "label": {"eng": "Ulysses S.
